              <span id="reviewTextContainer2944083982" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5768204949176988362">Basically, after reading a bunch of reviews of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/830502.It' title='It by Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>It</a> with all the child orgy stuff in them (and apologist comments that are ALWAYS left to debate ANY negative review of that novel), I wanted to see if <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7278752.Dolores_Claiborne' title='Dolores Claiborne by Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dolores Claiborne</a> still holds up. It's the novel of King's that I remember liking the most and it is about a woman who kills her husband because he sexually pursues his own daughter. It does hold up, mostly. It's an unputdownable psychological thriller told by a charismatic narrator. And evidently Kin</span>
  <span id="freeText5768204949176988362" style="display:none">Basically, after reading a bunch of reviews of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/830502.It" title="It by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">It</a> with all the child orgy stuff in them (and apologist comments that are ALWAYS left to debate ANY negative review of that novel), I wanted to see if <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7278752.Dolores_Claiborne" title="Dolores Claiborne by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Dolores Claiborne</a> still holds up. It's the novel of King's that I remember liking the most and it is about a woman who kills her husband because he sexually pursues his own daughter. It does hold up, mostly. It's an unputdownable psychological thriller told by a charismatic narrator. And evidently King does have an understanding of what child molestation and child objectification are. It's just for some reason, he conveniently forgot about these things (or was too drunk or high) while writing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/830502.It" title="It by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">It</a>. <br /><br />I am mystified by the strange paranormal connections between <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7278752.Dolores_Claiborne" title="Dolores Claiborne by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Dolores Claiborne</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32692.Gerald_s_Game" title="Gerald's Game by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Gerald's Game</a>. Maybe I'll read that next. Is it any good?</span>
  <a data-text-id="5768204949176988362" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31954777-chantal?shelf=book-cabinet">book-cabinet</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31954777-chantal?shelf=ebook-nl">ebook-nl</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31954777-chantal?shelf=kobo">kobo</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2974517974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6066833059740013386">Wow, what a great story! In the beginning I had a hard time making up what kind of setting it was and what was going on. After I found that out I started to love this story. I could not put the book down and I am happy that I have read it. Good character building and development. Great suspense building in general.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1580407481i/28874844.jpg' width='350' height='197' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/></span>
  <span id="freeText6066833059740013386" style="display:none">Wow, what a great story! In the beginning I had a hard time making up what kind of setting it was and what was going on.   <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1062730520" />
    <a title="Ruth Turner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17861817-ruth-turner"><img alt="Ruth Turner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435387851p2/17861817.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1062730520?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1062730520" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11906284510555914172"><br/>Audiobook – Narrated by Frances Sternhagen – Excellent Narration.<br/><br/>Written as one chapter, this is the story of Dolores Claiborne who is suspected of murdering her wealthy employer.<br/><br/>Instead, during one long night, in a police interview, she confesses to murdering her husband 30 years ago.<br/><br/>The story goes back in time as she tells about the woman she worked for and is suspected of killing, about her marriage and her violent and abusive husband, and her life on Little Tall Island. <br/><br/>I’ve read the book on</span>
  <span id="freeText11906284510555914172" style="display:none"><br />Audiobook – Narrated by Frances Sternhagen – Excellent Narration.<br /><br />Written as one chapter, this is the story of Dolores Claiborne who is suspected of murdering her wealthy employer.<br /><br />Instead, during one long night, in a police interview, she confesses to murdering her husband 30 years ago.<br /><br />The story goes back in time as she tells about the woman she worked for and is suspected of killing, about her marriage and her violent and abusive husband, and her life on Little Tall Island. <br /><br />I’ve read the book on a number of occasions over the years, and this is the second listening of the audio, and I seem to enjoy it more each time.<br /><br />Different than what we usually expect from Stephen King, but where it is the same, as with all his books, are the wonderfully rich, diverse characters that he introduces us to.<br /><br />I don’t particularly enjoy female narrators but Frances Sternhagen does a great job bringing to life this strong, gutsy, often foul mouthed but compassionate woman.<br /><br />***<br /><br /><br />CONNECTIONS, COINCIDENCES and CHESTNUTS:<br /><br />Dolores Claiborne and Gerald’s Game were written in tandem and were intended to be a two-volume set called In the Path of the Eclipse. To me, that would have made more sense than two stand-alone books with such a tenuous connection.<br /><br />“I thought of that little girl again, the one I told you about before, and all at once I saw her just as clear as day. She was down on her knees, too, lookin under her bed, and I thought, “She's so unhappy, and she smells that same smell. The one that's like pennies and oysters. Only it didn't come from the well; it has something to do with her father.” She’s speaking of Jessie (Gerald’s Game)<br /><br />***<br /><br />Little Tall Island (The Storm of the Century, Home Delivery)<br /><br />Derry mentioned<br /><br />The Reach (The Reach, Home Delivery)<br /><br />The Outer Islands (Home Delivery)<br /><br />Ellsworth (Home Delivery)<br /><br />The Island Princess (Home Delivery)<br /><br />Fudgy’s Tavern (Home Delivery)<br /><br />University of Maine in Orono (IT, Pet Semetery, The Tommyknockers, Gerald’s Game)<br /><br />Machias (Home Delivery)<br /><br />New Gloucester (Hearts in Atlantis) Also, Stephen King’s mother worked for a time as a housekeeper at Pineland, a mental institution in the town of New Gloucester.<br /><br />Jonesport (The Reach) mentioned.<br />Amusing: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nytimes.com/1993/09/17/news/at-the-bar-a-maine-lobsterman-s-justice-or-evoking-stephen-king-to-trap-a-debtor.html">http://www.nytimes.com/1993/09/17/new...</a><br /><br />Shawshank State Prison (Rita Hayworth and Shawshank Redemption, Apt Pupil, The Body, IT, The Sun Dog, Blaze, Bag of Bones, Under The Dome, 11/22/63) <br /><br />Big Clock Solitaire – Lloyd Henreid (The Stand) was cheating at Big Clock Solitaire in the Cub Bar.<br /><br />My Pretty Pony - While being questioned by Dr. McAuliffe about her husband's death, Dolores thinks to herself, "One, my-pretty-pony...two, my-pretty-pony...three, my-pretty-pony," to give herself time before answering the questions. Stephen King wrote a short story called My Pretty Pony, collected in Nightmares and Dreamscapes. It is also a Dollar Baby short film.<br /><br />Selena St George (Home Delivery)<br /><br />Dolores Claiborne (The Storm of the Century)<br /><br />Rita Hayworth, the actress, rates a mention.<br /><br />Atropos (Insomnia) possesses Gage Creed’s sneaker.<br /><br />Frank Proulx shares his surname with Mrs Proulx (Dedication) and also Annie Proulx, an author whose books, Close Range: Wyoming Stories and The Shipping News, made it to King’s List of 96 Books For Aspiring Writers To Read.<br /><br />Dr Freneau shares his surname with Irma and Tansy Freneau (Black House) and also with Philip Freneau, an American poet, who wrote a poem called “The Indian Burial Ground.” Interesting.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2352827151" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2352827151" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1376766-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582132250p2/1376766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2352827151?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1376766-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/28286938-dolores-claiborne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=bookclub_njsk">bookclub_njsk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=highly-recommended">highly-recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=mystery-and-thrillers">mystery-and-thrillers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2352827151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11736490646398332891">The last time (and first time, I think) I read this book I probably wasn't even old enough to vote, and so, in the grand scheme of Stephen King books, I wasn't super impressed. There wasn't really any horror to speak of, there wasn't any monster, there wasn't any hero to hope defeats (or at least survives) the monster, etc. Except there's all of those things, I was just not able to see them. That's not to say that I disliked it... It was a good book and Dolores' character was well done and memor</span>
  <span id="freeText11736490646398332891" style="display:none">The last time (and first time, I think) I read this book I probably wasn't even old enough to vote, and so, in the grand scheme of Stephen King books, I wasn't super impressed. There wasn't really any horror to speak of, there wasn't any monster, there wasn't any hero to hope defeats (or at least survives) the monster, etc. Except there's all of those things, I was just not able to see them. That's not to say that I disliked it... It was a good book and Dolores' character was well done and memorable, etc... but this book was just not one that something-teen year old me would have appreciated properly. <br /><br />I know. I was shocked too! Especially considering that there are still some books that I don't appreciate properly, but I digress. <br /><br />So anywho... many moons (and an eclipse!) later, and we read this for my Real-Life Bookclub, and, of course, it was an entirely different experience. For one, I'm no longer sharing a peer group with Selena, but now have the privilege of being the same age as Dolores is for most of the book's story. And while I've had a VERY different set of life experiences to Dolores, I'm much better able to identify with her, and actually understand this story. Maybe. <br /><br />Before we get into all of that, I want to just make a coupla quick comments about the writing style of the book. This is only the second (to my knowledge) of King's books that uses a (mostly) single narrative, sort of stream of consciousness style, meaning that there are no segments or chapters, no breaks in the narrative at all. Dolores is telling her story, and she means to do it straight through. Which is fine, and in this type of format it works, because the whole story is told from her perspective, in her way. There is a sort of add-on epilogue chapter that's outside of her narrative, but, honestly I think it would be just fine without it. <br /><br />The other book of King's to use this style is Cujo, and there it DOESN'T work (in my opinion), because Cujo is a story that jumps around and changes perspective and focus quite frequently, so it would actually benefit from being segmented. <br /><br />In both cases, though, it does feel like he's experimenting, trying something new with the narrative that he hasn't done before. Cujo is told in 3rd person omniscient style, as if an outsider is just following all of the relevant pieces to show the reader and give the full story, but doesn't ever take coffee breaks, so you I hope you peed before you started because we ain't stopping this ride. <br /><br />Dolores tells her story in first person, so we are literally only seeing that which she decides to show us, and once she starts talking, she doesn't stop until the story is told... but there are interruptions, and that's where I feel like this one gets "experiment-y". Lemme 'splain. <br /><br />Dolores is basically giving a deposition, and the story she tells is being recorded by a stenographer as her testimony regarding the suspicious death of her employer. Where it seems a little off is that we literally ONLY hear Dolores' voice. When the police or investigators interrupt, or ask questions, or when ANYONE else speaks, there's nothing to indicate them at all, except Dolores's reply. Which is weird. It's sort of using that dialogue-as-narrative trick that I hate, but in a different way. My point is that... I'm not sure whether we're supposed to feel like we're reading her deposition transcript, or if we're in the room with her as she's giving it, or if we're in her head. If it's either of the first two options, then I would expect for all of the conversation to be represented... and the 3rd option just doesn't feel right to me. (Though, actually, typing that out has made me think of this story in a different way... Hmm... OK... We'll come back to this.)<br /><br />Anyway, so narrative structure aside (for the moment), let's move on. So, Dolores is being questioned because everyone in town thinks that she killed her employer, because everyone in town is pretty sure that she killed her husband thirty years ago and got away with it. Knowing that she's in a pretty tight spot, she decides to 'fess up, and tells the story of how, and why, she did in fact kill her husband, and how her bosslady came to be dead, but that it wasn't murder. <br /><br />Dolores says that every story has layers. There's the story that's on top, for all the world to see, or see what they want or expect to see, and then somewhere buried underneath is the truth. Her story, and this book, is like that. <br /><br />We can take Dolores at face value, and trust that what she's saying is the truth. At this point, what other option does she have but to come clean? She's 65 years old, her kids are adults and successful and independent, and town society won't let her keep herself to herself... not when, for the second time, a difficult person that Dolores lived with for many years has ended up suspiciously dead. <br /><br />It's easy to want to take her at her word. She shows us how she's lived a hard life, with an abusive, semi-alcoholic husband, three kids, a plethora of hard, manual labor jobs, the primary of which is for a woman who fires other maids when the wind blows. She shows us that she's skimped and saved and sacrificed to try to give her kids a better life than she ended up with. She shows us that she doesn't shy away from hard work or hard decisions... She shows us that she's willing to put up with a lot... but not a husband who, after 15 years of marriage, decided he might like to try a younger model, and started grooming and manipulating and touching their 14 year old daughter. She found her line in the sand. <br /><br />Now, in that situation, I maybe might've jumped straight to Googling "how to clean human blood out of a woodchipper"... But, no, Dolores is a better woman than I. She went a different way and just tried to stop the situation first by confronting him, with an escape route Plan B... But life has a way of fucking you over, and Dolores is no fool. She might've intimidated &amp; threatened her dear husband into compliance temporarily, but she knows that he's a snake and cannot be trusted. Her daughter would never be safe with him, and that's Dolores's primary goal. <br /><br />It's easy to understand that kind of motivation. And my summary doesn't do it justice... Her version is comprehensive and compelling. Mine is like a stick figure drawing next to her Mona Lisa. It's easy to believe and agree with her story, and that he needed to die, and that she did, really, the only thing that she could've in that situation, in that time, in that community, that would guarantee her daughter never had to worry about waking up in the middle of the night with daddy standing next to her bed with his hands where they should not be. <br /><br />You don't confess to a 30 year old murder, even the kind that was ostensibly necessary to protect your family, lightly. She did so to show that she has nothing to hide, and to lend credence to her claim that she didn't kill her boss. But of course, that's assuming that Dolores is telling the truth. <br /><br />We only get her perspective. There's no interviewing anyone else, like her daughter, to ask if any of the claims Dolores made were true, there's no investigation, nothing. The story, from start to finish (except the epilogue) is whatever she wants us to believe. It all sounds plausible, and believable. It all sounds justifiable. But we really don't know. She could have killed him for any number of reasons. He called her ugly one too many times, or she was tired of cleaning the skidmarks out of his underwear, or she just was tired of looking at his stupid face. It could be anything. And since she got away with it for 30 years, there's been plenty of time for the truth's edges to wear down and become whatever she wants them to be. <br /><br />Of course, the 3rd option is that none of this is real and she's as nutty as squirrel poop and making up all of it, the murders, the deposition, all of it. I mean, that would explain why there are no other voices in the narrative... they don't exist. She's talking to herself, and we get to be privy to only the one side of the conversation. <br /><br />This option makes complete sense when you think about it. Vera lived in the same kind of delusional bubble, right? She was "estranged" from her kids, and made up all sorts of reasons why they never came to see her, to the point where she believed them herself. It's not so far-fetched for Dolores to have done the same, only with her reasons for killing her husband, and then her boss... only she isn't taking the wrap for that one... not even in her own head. <br /><br />I don't really believe that option 3 is realistic, though. For one, there's not really anything that's a plausible enough candidate for her to have snapped and lost touch with reality, the way that Vera did. Secondly, we know that at least part of the tale is true, because the epilogue features a snippet of news about part of Vera's will endowment. So I guess it can't really all be in her head, but wouldn't it have been fun? Serious, stoic, plodding, and resolute Dolores being all bats in the belfry. But no. Sigh. <br /><br />But still, who's to say she's not completely sane and rational and playing all of us for fools? She makes a compelling argument, but we don't HAVE to believe her just on the basis of her word... Who am I kidding, I believe her. The story sucks otherwise, because it's meaningless if it's all just a bunch of lies, rather than the love of a mother doing what she had to do. <br /><br />OK, moving on! This book was released in 1992 but most of it takes place in 1962-63, and it definitely FEELS like it. There was just a general sexism that hung over the whole narrative - from the role that Dolores felt was hers to take on as mother, wife, cook, cleaning lady, etc, on top of working 12-14 hour days as well, because it was expected, to the attitudes of men, specifically the bank manager, acting like women are incapable of practicality or sense at all, and therefore aren't worthy of thought or consideration regarding decisions. (Though, I guess there are men who still think that.)<br /><br />The scene in the bank was so frustrating to me, because poor Dolores was optionless and stuck, and it was just so maddeningly ingrained sexist bureaucratic bullshit. But Dolores being Dolores, she took it in stride and started to make her Plan C. It's just so shitty that women were considered so trivial that she didn't even warrant a single phone call to inform her of the change to the account that she was co-owner of. Not a single thought that maybe she might be interested in what's happening with the thousands of dollars in her kids' savings accounts. Maddening to have so little control over your own life and money and freedom. (Also, this is the reason that I don't have joint bank accounts with ANYONE.) <br /><br />Speaking of the kids, it was much easier for me to see and understand the relationships (or lack thereof) between Dolores and her kids. Everything that she did was for them. Everything. She broke her back day in and day out for them, to try to give them a better life and better opportunity, and at every turn something was there to trip her up, and most of the times that thing was named Joe. <br /><br />Selena in particular, being the oldest and the only girl, was especially easy for me to identify with this read through. Having been the oldest, and only girl, and the benefit of having lived through my teen years and now being eye to eye with Dolores (age wise) it's so much easier to see their relationship and all of the ways that it was dysfunctional. Selena needs a mother, but what she has is a housekeeper doing rounds in her house as well as a dozen others. When she IS there, there's always some sort of argument or antagonizing situation going on that, from a child/teen perspective, is unreasonable and scary, like when Dolores finally confronts Joe about hitting her, hatchet in hand. So it's easy to see how Joe could take advantage of Selena's confusion and fear regarding her mother, and twist that into something he can use to his own selfish, depraved advantage. Dolores says (paraphrasing): "All I wanted to do is make him stop, and I risked my life to do it, but she didn't see none of that. Everything she saw was stacked up on his side of the ledger."<br /><br />There was a passage in the book, after Selena had finally shared her secret with her mom, that broke my heart because it's so cruel of her father to manipulate her this way, as though she is responsible for him touching her. "She didn't understand how bringing him a glass of iced tea in the afternoon and telling him about Laurie Langill's new puppy had given him the idea that he could reach between her legs and squeeze her there whenever he wanted, but she was convinced she must have done something to make him act so bad, and it made her ashamed."<br /><br />How terrifying that must have been for her, being stuck in a house with a father trying to rape her, and having his warnings that should she find out what "they" were up to (as if she was a willing participant), Dolores would kill them both. And Selena, having seen her mom standing over Joe with a hatchet in hand believed it. I can't even imagine what that would be like, and it makes me appreciate the scene on the ferry so much more, because poor Selena was like a cornered cat, terrified and lashing out, but Dolores took it all, as she takes everything, and her persistence and patience and calmness won out and Selena was able to unburden herself. What a relief it must have been for her. <br /><br />This book and Gerald's Game were both released the same year, and are tied together by the father molestation storyline, as well as the solar eclipse. In a way, these two books can kind of be seen as alternate versions of the molestation. One, where the abuse is found out and stopped, and one where the girl has nobody to protect her. Dolores is somehow able to see Jessie being molested by her father during the eclipse (maybe the eclipse opened a thinny or perhaps formed a link between her and Jessie? maybe a tiny bit of the Shine or the Touch?), and I think that this galvanizes her to follow through on her plan to kill him. The only problem with that is that old wells don't make good murder weapons. <br /><br />Anyway... there's a lot going on in this book, considering that at less than 400 pages it's actually one of King's shorter books, and I could keep ramblin' on, but it's now late here and I should get to bed. I'm glad that I re-read this book as an adult, because I got so much more out of it this time around. I don't know if this is my favorite portrayal of a female main character from King, honestly that might have to be Lisey Landon... but Dolores is right up there. She's a trooper.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11736490646398332891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1976866919" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1976866919" />
    <a title="Saif Sayed " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64785457-saif-sayed"><img alt="Saif Sayed " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530356912p2/64785457.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1976866919?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Saif Sayed " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Saif Sayed " href="/user/show/64785457-saif-sayed">Saif Sayed </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781444707441" href="/book/show/11740941-dolores-claiborne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64785457-saif-sayed?shelf=books-read-in-2017">books-read-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64785457-saif-sayed?shelf=stephenking">stephenking</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1976866919" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer505718675719878778">This novel was under the further reading section of Sharp Objects by Gillian Flynn which lured me to buy it. (I was new to Stephen King back then)<br/><br/>Dolores Claiborne is basically a confession of a woman named by the title, to the police after being accused of killing her employer Vera Donavan. Although she didn't kill Vera, she did confess the things she's been through past 30 years of being with her that was buried deep under herself all this time. <br/><br/>This novel is full of fast paced suspense with a</span>
  <span id="freeText505718675719878778" style="display:none">This novel was under the further reading section of Sharp Objects by Gillian Flynn which lured me to buy it. (I was new to Stephen King back then)<br /><br />Dolores Claiborne is basically a confession of a woman named by the title, to the police after being accused of killing her employer Vera Donavan. Although she didn't kill Vera, she did confess the things she's been through past 30 years of being with her that was buried deep under herself all this time. <br /><br />This novel is full of fast paced suspense with an enthralling premise. There are no chapters, and it's more or less written as a huge monologue. Dolores is one of the strongest character I have come across. It's was so depressing at the same time infuriating to think of the things she's been through and the way she dealt with it was completely remarkable. <br /><br />As she say it, "Fool me once, shame on you. Fool me twice, shame on me."<br /><br />I am totally impressed by the writing. The way King voices woman characters is commendable, be it a teenage girl or a savage old woman, King is the best when it comes to writing powerful and complex characters. <br /><br />Highly recommended to those who love suspense and thrillers with spine chilling background!</span>
  <a data-text-id="505718675719878778" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_139920265" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139920265" />
    <a title="Book Concierge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1628041-book-concierge"><img alt="Book Concierge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324904606p2/1628041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/139920265?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book Concierge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book Concierge" href="/user/show/1628041-book-concierge">Book Concierge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670844524" href="/book/show/1082102.Dolores_Claiborne">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Book Concierge by: </span>
      Clara Zukrow
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=concierge">concierge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=movie">movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=maine">maine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=strong-women">strong-women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer139920265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer95326394994794730">5***** and a ❤<br/><br/>The entire novel is told by Dolores Claiborne in her sworn statement to the investigating police. Her employer of 40 years has died in a tragic accident … or did Dolores kill her? Dolores doesn’t pull any punches, she tells us on page two that she did <u>
  <b>not</b>
</u> kill Vera Donovan (though Vera deserved killing, more than once … she was a high-riding bitch). No, Dolores did nothing to hasten old Mrs Donovan’s ending … but she <u>
  <b>did</b>
</u> kill her husband, Joe St George, twenty-nine years ag</span>
  <span id="freeText95326394994794730" style="display:none">5***** and a ❤<br /><br />The entire novel is told by Dolores Claiborne in her sworn statement to the investigating police. Her employer of 40 years has died in a tragic accident … or did Dolores kill her? Dolores doesn’t pull any punches, she tells us on page two that she did <u>
  <b>not</b>
</u> kill Vera Donovan (though Vera deserved killing, more than once … she was a high-riding bitch). No, Dolores did nothing to hasten old Mrs Donovan’s ending … but she <u>
  <b>did</b>
</u> kill her husband, Joe St George, twenty-nine years ago. <br /><br />King is a master plotter and he moves the story along at a fair clip. Slowing down at just the right places to let the reader catch his breath. Scaring you the next minute with the horrors of human meanness. Making you laugh at the ridiculous things people do. And letting you into Dolores’s mind – a nearly 66-year-old woman who has had a hard life and who isn’t about to suffer fools gladly. She’s tired and ready to get everything off her chest. <br /><br />Dolores has spent her life on Little Tall Island. She’s worked hard, keeping house for rich Mrs Donovan, and caring for her in her senility. She’s raised three children without much help from Joe, a no-good alcoholic husband who beat her one time too many. But you need to get the whole story straight from Dolores. <br /><br />I first read this in 1997. What really impressed me was that King manages to write in a thick Maine accent you can “hear” on the page. (And the audio book, narrated by Frances Sternhagen, is nothing short of wonderful!) Dolores will stay with you for a long time. King has managed to write a character who is not very likable, but whom I just fell in love with. Bravo!</span>
  <a data-text-id="95326394994794730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1650630568" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1650630568" />
    <a title="Erin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32799998-erin"><img alt="Erin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469767858p2/32799998.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1650630568?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin" href="/user/show/32799998-erin">Erin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32799998-erin?shelf=movie">movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32799998-erin?shelf=december-2019">december-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32799998-erin?shelf=king-me">king-me</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1650630568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5859489481470389078">Dolores Claiborne is weird little book. <br/><br/>I don't quite know how to explain it. <br/><br/>First it has no chapters or page breaks. <br/><br/>It's more of extra long monologue, than a novel. <br/><br/>Dolores Claiborne has had a hard life. She married a drunk who couldn't keep a job, when she was 18 years old because she was pregnant. She's spent the last almost 40 years working as a housekeeper for a very wealthy and mean old lady. She's estranged from her kids. She's just not a very happy person. <br/><br/>The novel consists of Dolores</span>
  <span id="freeText5859489481470389078" style="display:none">Dolores Claiborne is weird little book. <br /><br />I don't quite know how to explain it. <br /><br />First it has no chapters or page breaks. <br /><br />It's more of extra long monologue, than a novel. <br /><br />Dolores Claiborne has had a hard life. She married a drunk who couldn't keep a job, when she was 18 years old because she was pregnant. She's spent the last almost 40 years working as a housekeeper for a very wealthy and mean old lady. She's estranged from her kids. She's just not a very happy person. <br /><br />The novel consists of Dolores Claiborne sitting in a police station telling the police her life story. <br /><br />Why is she telling the police her life story? <br /><br />Because they think she murdered the mean old lady she worked for. <br /><br />Why do they think that? <br /><br />Because the whole town thinks she murdered her husband 30 years earlier. <br /><br />This book isn't a horror novel but the subject matter is horrifying. Its Stephen King so ALL THE TRIGGER WARNINGS! This book is DARK! Its a hard book to read at times. Stephen King creates such an alive and vivid world solely through Dolores' words. Despite the fact that Dolores is simply talking us through her life, I could see and feel every experience she described. <br /><br />This book really surprised me. I enjoyed much more than expected. I only read Dolores Claiborne because I heard it was a kind of companion book to Gerald's Game and I wanted to know how they were connected. I'm glad I read this book and I look forward to watching the movie...someday.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5859489481470389078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer6064942256802884532">I read this in almost one day. If you're an audiobook type of person, I highly recommend the audiobook version for this one. The book has a lot of dialect that the narrator absolutely nailed. This story all takes place as Dolores Claiborne is being questioned for the death of her wealthy boss, but instead confesses to killing her husband during an eclipse several years earlier. There is some great production where you can hear coffee being poured and small things like that. It made the experienc</span>
  <span id="freeText6064942256802884532" style="display:none">I read this in almost one day. If you're an audiobook type of person, I highly recommend the audiobook version for this one. The book has a lot of dialect that the narrator absolutely nailed. This story all takes place as Dolores Claiborne is being questioned for the death of her wealthy boss, but instead confesses to killing her husband during an eclipse several years earlier. There is some great production where you can hear coffee being poured and small things like that. It made the experience very immersive. This was a great, quick read and Dolores is quite a character. This one has it's obvious connections to Gerald's Game, and it's quite different from a usual King book. </span>

<div id="review_1212631244" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1212631244" />
    <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt"><img alt="Rebecca McNutt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602390057p2/36977686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1212631244?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca McNutt" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt">Rebecca McNutt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9789024536993" href="/book/show/3187672-dolores-claiborne">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1212631244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14341096524412464756">Dolores Claiborne is one of my favorite Stephen King books; not only does it step away from horror a little bit but it manages to keep it's "Stephen King style" while being unique from his other horror novels (which are excellent but often follow the same formula). <br/><br/>Dolores Claiborne is dreary, tragic and features a woman so plain, homely and traditional in appearance that she's almost unmemorable... but as the novel reveals the secrets of Dolores' twisted, jerk of a husband and unstable daughter</span>
  <span id="freeText14341096524412464756" style="display:none">Dolores Claiborne is one of my favorite Stephen King books; not only does it step away from horror a little bit but it manages to keep it's "Stephen King style" while being unique from his other horror novels (which are excellent but often follow the same formula). <br /><br />Dolores Claiborne is dreary, tragic and features a woman so plain, homely and traditional in appearance that she's almost unmemorable... but as the novel reveals the secrets of Dolores' twisted, jerk of a husband and unstable daughter, it left me always thinking back to the people on the street and in town every day... the old crossing guard near my house, the overweight cashier at the grocery store, the weathered looking mom dressed in frumpy clothes and carrying a baby... Dolores Claiborne left me wondering how many of these people have their own sinister secrets hidden from the outside world. <br /><br />This is one of King's only novels that will take you behind closed doors into the life of a woman whose daughter was abused, whose husband was a pig and whose best friend was her snobby employer who knew what it was like to want revenge and respect. Is it scary? Does it have ghouls with sunken eyes or evil clowns or vampires? Nope, it doesn't have any of that, but it's scary because it brings out the monsters that are in people, monsters like men who beat their wives and cops who just won't listen. Dolores Claiborne brings out the fear lurking in everyday family life and small towns where secrets hardly ever surface.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14341096524412464756" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2617615773" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2617615773" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2617615773?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670844524" href="/book/show/1082102.Dolores_Claiborne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2617615773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7075845714046240208">This is more of an OK than a good book for me. <br/><br/>What kind of book is this and why did I choose to try this one of the many written by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King' title='Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stephen King</a>? First of all, because it is not a book of the paranormal and not a book of horror. It is instead a psychological thriller. <br/><br/>Dolores Claiborne is an outspoken and strongly opinionated widow of sixty-five working as a housekeeper for Vera Donovan, a wealthy resident of a small island community in Maine. Dolores is Vera’s housekeeper. She began working fo</span>
  <span id="freeText7075845714046240208" style="display:none">This is more of an OK than a good book for me. <br /><br />What kind of book is this and why did I choose to try this one of the many written by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King" title="Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Stephen King</a>? First of all, because it is not a book of the paranormal and not a book of horror. It is instead a psychological thriller. <br /><br />Dolores Claiborne is an outspoken and strongly opinionated widow of sixty-five working as a housekeeper for Vera Donovan, a wealthy resident of a small island community in Maine. Dolores is Vera’s housekeeper. She began working for Vera in 1949. Now, in 1992, Vera has died and some say it is Dolores that has murdered her. The postman rang at the door, Dolores opens and there on the floor he sees Vera sprawled inanimate and bloody with a marble rolling pin lying nearby. Vera is old and demented and confined to a wheel chair; it certainly does not look good for Dolores. The next day, not to make things better, Dolores is informed by Vera’s attorney that she is to inherit millions from Vera. You think perhaps this is good news but think twice. This makes her look even more guilty! What does she do? In the afternoon of the same day she goes to the police and spills out her guts. The transcript of her “statement”, the spilling out of her guts to the police officers, is this book. This explains why it reads as a monologue.<br /><br />So, suspected of one murder, what does Dolores do but go and confess to having committed another. She speaks not only of her life as Vera’s housekeeper but also of how and why she came to murder her husband almost thirty years ago. Tell me, suspected of one murder do you go and confess to another? Sorry, this does not make sense to me! But as I said, she was spilling her guts! <br /><br />The book, as a transcript of Dolores’ statement made on the afternoon of Vera’s death, is too long. I listened to this statement for more than nine hours, the length of the audiobook. All of this was said in one afternoon?<br /><br />I have problems with the feasibility of the events as they are described. I am a stickler for realism. So far two things do not make sense, and there are more.<br /><br />I was looking for a reason which would explain why Dolores would confess now to the earlier murder. All I could come up with was that she knew how Vera was haunted by misdeeds <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ similar to her own, in the form of “dust- bunnies” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Still, that Dolores should blurt everything out now does not make sense. Her ability to recall every detail of the events thirty years earlier doesn’t make sense either. She draws a minute by minute account. She goes off on all sorts of tangents. The telling loses credibility. <br /><br />The end, well let’s put it this way, it’s fictional. Dolores is <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[willed millions and gives it all away to charity <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />And here is another problem. While I got a kick out of the grumbling friendship that grew between Dolores and Vera, the two came to care for each other and yet constantly harassed one another, it amazes me that Vera did not seem to understand Dolores enough to know that Dolores would not want <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Vera’s money <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>! What kind of friendship is that?<br /> <br />The monologue is drawn in a gimmicky fashion. Short interruptions are made for the pouring of a glass of water and finally whiskey stashed away in the officer’s desk. You actually hear the drink being poured. Once or twice, Dolores breaks off and responds to a question posed by an officer, but for the most part the two police officers and a stenographer say absolutely nothing.<br /><br />Moments of suspense are added, as in a movie. Someone is climbing up a <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[well <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The person falls back and mounts again. Will the person escape, and will the person succeed in grabbing <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Dolores <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>? This type of suspense has been done umpteen times before! Moaning and screaming accompany this event. I laughed. I was not scared.<br /><br />I do not find the book credible, except for one point—that Dolores was determined to do what she thought was best for her children. <br /><br />The narration by Frances Sternhagen is absolutely excellent. I mean fantastic. Her performance is worthy of five stars. The voice she gives Dolores is p-e-r-f-e-c-t. It is to her credit that I was drawn into the book at the start. As I got into the book though, its lack of credibility gave me problems. <br /><br />********************<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11566.The_Green_Mile" title="The Green Mile by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Green Mile</a> 4 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1082102.Dolores_Claiborne" title="Dolores Claiborne by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Dolores Claiborne</a> 2 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="7075845714046240208" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2053337355" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2053337355" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24154009-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578362761p2/24154009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2053337355?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/24154009-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670844524" href="/book/show/1082102.Dolores_Claiborne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24154009-jason?shelf=stephen-king">stephen-king</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2053337355" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer788428189312061857">Middle of the pack King book, for me.<br/><br/>"There is no bitch on earth like a mother frightened for her kids." If the book were to be distilled down into one sentence, this would do it justice. <br/><br/>I can appreciate that King was doing something different and unique here with the narration. He was definitely taking himself out of his own comfort zone. And in King fashion, he pulled it off (surprise surprise). I think this narrative style could have been a complete disaster if it wasn't a pro doing the work</span>
  <span id="freeText788428189312061857" style="display:none">Middle of the pack King book, for me.<br /><br />"There is no bitch on earth like a mother frightened for her kids." If the book were to be distilled down into one sentence, this would do it justice. <br /><br />I can appreciate that King was doing something different and unique here with the narration. He was definitely taking himself out of his own comfort zone. And in King fashion, he pulled it off (surprise surprise). I think this narrative style could have been a complete disaster if it wasn't a pro doing the work. I did, however, find it a bit difficult to read at times, if only briefly. That's probably just a result of not being used to reading something where the first-person narration is Claiborne-esque. I don't know any other way of describing the voice other than "Claiborne-esque." It was a bit dicey, a bit choppy, didn't really flow all that well at times. But I'll chalk that up to Claiborne's voice itself and not King.<br /><br />I didn't really find the story to be all that captivating. It just didn't have the same pull and tension that I find King usually has in his stories. The outcome was very predictable, so I was underwhelmed when I read what I expected to inevitably happen. There was a bit of a soft and sweet ending, but nothing so much as to draw any real and teary-eyed emotion out of me.<br /><br />Claiborne herself was fine. But I actually liked all the secondary characters more and wished I'd seen more of them, in particular Claiborne's daughter, husband, and Vera. I think if I'd have gotten to know them better, I'd have cared more about the way the story unfolded.<br /><br />There's also the big question, which is: Is Dolores Claiborne guilty, or was she just doing what any mother would do? The book has a very "A Time to Kill" feel to it, with a very similar outcome. I guess the book is trying to raise questions of morality, namely: Is murder excusable when you're trying to protect yourself or your family via getting revenge for previous harm done? <br /><br />There was also the running motif of the Solar Eclipse, which I'm sure was supposed to symbolise something meaningful but sort of got lost in the more important points of the story. <br /><br />Truth is, I was mostly looking forward to finishing this novel so I could get to another King. Onward!</span>
  <a data-text-id="788428189312061857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('60tZf6DAD9b6EjuNY6WPY6kQfVB1ca+sfPrl6kMvOPANdBKcFyiBlQnrU+zQwkQPWYZt//wORPCB2sGr5KHfdA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bPhEXu9e0DaH2ANCSf4s60EHkS6MeBYyd/Pp0A+jvzmKxw+9WLZedXQhayP6meeHsZGBgQUH/W6K082RqC1YvQ==')})
  }

Yet Henrietta\'s family did not learn of her \'immortality\' until more than twenty years after her death, with devastating consequences . . .Rebecca Skloot\'s fascinating account is the story of the life, and afterlife, of one woman who changed the medical world forever. Why I\'m No Longer Talking to White People About Race: The book that sparked a national conversation. Exploring everything from eradicated black history to the inextricable link between class and race, Why I\'m No Longer Talking to White People About Race is the essential handbook for anyone who wants       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3389.Stephen_King">About Stephen King</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15305178400989224231">Stephen Edwin King was born the second son of Donald and Nellie Ruth Pillsbury King. After his father left them when Stephen was two, he and his older brother, David, were raised by his mother. Parts of his childhood were spent in Fort Wayne, Indiana, where his father's family was at the time, and in Stratford, Connecticut. When Stephen was eleven, his mother brought her children back to Durham, M</span>
  <span id="freeText15305178400989224231" style="display:none">Stephen Edwin King was born the second son of Donald and Nellie Ruth Pillsbury King. After his father left them when Stephen was two, he and his older brother, David, were raised by his mother. Parts of his childhood were spent in Fort Wayne, Indiana, where his father's family was at the time, and in Stratford, Connecticut. When Stephen was eleven, his mother brought her children back to Durham, Maine, for good. Her parents, Guy and Nellie Pillsbury, had become incapacitated with old age, and Ruth King was persuaded by her sisters to take over the physical care of them. Other family members provided a small house in Durham and financial support. After Stephen's grandparents passed away, Mrs. King found work in the kitchens of Pineland, a nearby residential facility for the mentally challenged.<br /><br />Stephen attended the grammar school in Durham and Lisbon Falls High School, graduating in 1966. From his sophomore year at the University of Maine at Orono, he wrote a weekly column for the school newspaper, THE MAINE CAMPUS. He was also active in student politics, serving as a member of the Student Senate. He came to support the anti-war movement on the Orono campus, arriving at his stance from a conservative view that the war in Vietnam was unconstitutional. He graduated in 1970, with a B.A. in English and qualified to teach on the high school level. A draft board examination immediately post-graduation found him 4-F on grounds of high blood pressure, limited vision, flat feet, and punctured eardrums.<br /><br />He met Tabitha Spruce in the stacks of the Fogler Library at the University, where they both worked as students; they married in January of 1971. As Stephen was unable to find placement as a teacher immediately, the Kings lived on his earnings as a laborer at an industrial laundry, and her student loan and savings, with an occasional boost from a short story sale to men's magazines.<br /><br />Stephen made his first professional short story sale ("The Glass Floor") to <i>Startling Mystery Stories</i> in 1967. Throughout the early years of his marriage, he continued to sell stories to men's magazines. Many were gathered into the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10628.Night_Shift" title="Night Shift" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Shift</a> collection or appeared in other anthologies.<br /><br />In the fall of 1971, Stephen began teaching English at Hampden Academy, the public high school in Hampden, Maine. Writing in the evenings and on the weekends, he continued to produce short stories and to work on novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15305178400989224231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
